How do you make black buttercream
Pretty interesting
*150 g squeezable unsalted butter* - Beat for 5 minutes *340 g powder sugar* - Sort out through sieve - Add 1/2 of sugar to butter - Add second half of sugar when the mixture is nice, smooth, pale and whippy (cca 3-5 minutes) - Mix another 3-5 minutes (preferably 5) - Mix *1/4 tsp of vanilla extract* with *3 or 4 tbsp of whole milk* - Add just as much vanilla milk to your mixture as you need - With the vanilla milk add any flavours and/or colors you'd like - Speed the mixer up and beat for 3 or 4 minutes, just so the mixture connects well with the vanilla milk (and flavours and colors) - After a few minutes scrape the sides and check consistency, if need, add milk, continue mixing

HERES THE WHOLE RECIPE AND INSTRUCTIONS  Recipe for Buttercream:  2/3 C Butter 2 3/4 C Powdered Sugar (Sifted) 3 TBS Milk  Use softened butter, beat butter for 5 minutes until whipped and fluffy. In a separate bowl sift all your powdered sugar. Slowly incorporate the powdered sugar into the butter half of it at a time and let beat for 5 min each time. When finished, add in bits of milk until perfect consistency.

Perfect buttercream for 12 cupcakes -150g unsalted soft butter Beat for about 5 minutes until pale, smooth and silky  -340g icing/powdered sugar (soft) Sift at least once prior to use. Add half the icing sugar they beat for about 3-4 minutes so incorporated and then repeat. (Beat on slow to mix briefly then mix on medium to high speed).  If it is too stiff you need to add liquid i.e 1/4 teasooon vanilla extract and/or 2-3 tablespoons full cream milk. Add liquid in parts in case you add too much. Beat again for 3-4 minutes so all mixed in.   If you want to add a colour, colour paste is best as it is more concentrated and won't change the consistency of the icing. If you want it all the same colour add it in with the milk and the mixer will mix it all for you. If you are doing multiple colours, mix the paste with a small amount of separate buttercream then add to the bowl as it is easier to mix it in (the paste can be quite thick).  Always keep it covered when not using. This buttercream can be stored for up to 5 days in an airtight container if required.

Thanks for the video.  I make loads of buttercream for macarons which require different flavorings (raspberry, strawberry, blueberry, pistachio, nutella, the list goes on).  What I've found is that the buttercream technique is basically a trial and error technique to get the right amount of flavor, color, and consistency.  Each flavoring requires a differing ingredients levels and you basically have to work with it to get it to come out right.  This video for me, in an of itself, does not produce the "perfect" buttercream but is a good place to start.    Just one other note, I live in the south in the US and you definitely can't leave your butter out "overnight" especially in the spring and summer and use it straight up -- the recipe will totally fail.  The temperature and the consistency of the butter when you begin the cream in my opinion is single most important characteristic of the method.  This will highly depend upon the temperature of your kitchen.

(I'm in the US) I went to a specialty European market for reasons totally unrelated to baking and I found that same brand of French butter! Finally, I can make similar buttercream to yours :) Wish I could have found it earlier lol  I followed this recipe for buttercream for a cake I made last month, but my buttercream came out with a bunch of air-pockets. After watching the video again now, Jemma says "regardless of how much icing I'm making, I always add [the sugar] in 2 stages". I added the sugar in 3-4 stages and whipped 3 minutes in between each stage. Basically I added too much air which caused those air-pockets. Lesson learned! Hope this helps somebody else.

Some hints for those struggling with the metric measurements.  Grams is a measurement of weight. Cups is a measurement of volume. So if you do a direct conversion, you'll wind up with too much sugar, because sugar weighs more!   There are electronic food scales that are super cheap and handy that can work with ounces as well as grams. I have an OXO Good Grips stainless steel food scale and it can measure grams. Handy if you're looking at international recipes.  Now, I've been struggling with cupcakes that sunk in the middle as well as perhaps felt a bit too oily. The things I'll try is not use an unsalted butter with such a high butterfat content. I tend to use french butter with high milkfat content and I think that's contributing to some of it (but man, it tastes so good).  Unsalted butter in London, she's going to find butter that's a minimum of 80% milkfat  Another thing to point out for those dealing with sunken cupcakes.  An metric teaspoon is not the same as a US teaspoon. Egadzooks!  1 Imperial/British teaspoon is equal to just about 1.2 us teaspoon.  Which means if you put in 1 teaspoon of baking soda, you didn't put in enough. And we all know baking is really about exact measurements when it comes to getting your goods to rise.  So 1/4 teaspoon that Jemma says may very well be too little, if you're using a US teaspoon.  So how the heck do you measure .30 of a us teaspoon aka, 3/10th?  I'm going to "mound" my teaspoon with extra baking soda and see if that helps as well.  First things first, I'm going to go try a slightly more amount of baking soda to try and reach that imperial teaspoon and see if that helps.

Bit of help for everybody who has difficulty finding top quality ingredients. My go to place is actually Lidl! (UK based info or anywhere which has Lidl, apologies) Lidl British Unsalted butter is basically British Lescure - perfect for baking. It has an 82% fat content, like Lescure and it is creamy soft and tastes much butter than Sainsbury's or ASDA's and it's only 95p per 250g block. Icing sugar, Tate and Lyle uses 'Maize Starch' but it's dry, lumpy and when supermarkets finally get it off of their shelves it's old and crusty. Lidl icing sugar also uses 'Maize Starch' and it is softer, smoother and less dry and crusty and it is only 65p for a 500g box. Only buy Tate and Lyle if it's a commercial purchase because it is most likely to be fresher brought that way. Obviously milk is milk just make sure it's whole, I'm not going to tell you where to buy your milk. Lidl sell vanilla pods for less than Â£2 I believe or I use vanilla bean paste which I don't buy from Lidl. Vanilla is the only expensive ingredient really, extract and paste both ranging between Â£4-8 if it is a good quality kind. I hope this helps some of your buttercream nightmares.

I just created an awesome butter tip! If your butter is frozen, or in the fridge, take your butter and place it in a ziplock bag without any air! Then fill a tall glass with hot/ warm water and place it in the glass. I use a small bowl to keep the butter down in the water. And even with frozen butter. It takes only 20 minutes to soften. It feels like it has been sitting out all night!
